Y.", "Broadhurst, Tom", "Lim, Jeremy", "Diego, Jose M.", "Zitrin, Adi", "Coe, Dan", "Ford, Holland C."], "author_address": ["AA(Department of Physics, The University of Hong Kong, Pokfulam Road, Hong Kong), AB(Department of Theoretical Physics, University of Basque Country UPV/EHU, Bilbao, Spain; IKERBASQUE, Basque Foundation for Science, Bilbao, Spain;), AC(Department of Physics, The University of Hong Kong, Pokfulam Road, Hong Kong; Laboratory for Space Research, Faculty of Science, The University of Hong Kong, Pokfulam Road, Hong Kong), AD(IFCA, Instituto de F\u00edsica de Cantabria (UC-CSIC), Av. de Los Castros s/n, E-39005 Santander, Spain), AE(Cahill Center for Astronomy and Astrophysics, California Institute of Technology, MC 249-17, Pasadena, CA 91125, USA;), AF(Space Telescope Science Institute, Baltimore, MD, USA), AG(Department of Physics and Astronomy, Johns Hokpins University, Baltimore, Maryland, USA)"], "journal_name": "The Astrophysical Journal", "volume": "835", "publication_year": "2017/01/1", "start_page": "44", "keywords": ["dark matter", "galaxies: high-redshift", "gravitational lensing: strong", "Astrophysics - Cosmology and Nongalactic Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...835...44C", "abstract": "A multiply lensed galaxy, MACS0647-JD, with a probable photometric redshift of z\u2243 {10.7}<SUB>-0.4</SUB><SUP>+0.6</SUP> is claimed to constitute one of the very earliest known galaxies, formed well before reionization was completed. However, spectral evidence that MACS0647-JD lies at high redshift has proven infeasible and so here we seek an independent-lensing-based \u201cgeometric redshift\u201d derived from the angles between the three lensed images of MACS0647-JD, using our free-form mass model (WSLAP+) for the lensing cluster MACSJ0647.7+7015 (at z = 0.591). Our lens model uses the nine sets of multiple images, including those of MACS0647-JD, identified by the CLASH survey toward this cluster. We convincingly exclude the low-redshift regime of z &lt; 3, for which convoluted critical curves are generated by our method, as the solution bends to accommodate the wide angles of MACS0647-JD for this low redshift. Instead, a best fit to all sets of lensed galaxy positions and redshifts provides a geometric redshift of z\u2243 {10.8}<SUB>-0.4</SUB><SUP>+0.3</SUP> for MACS0647-JD, strongly supporting the higher photometric redshift solution. Importantly, we find a tight linear relation between the relative brightnesses of all nine sets of multiply lensed images and their relative magnifications as predicted by our model. This agreement provides a benchmark for the quality of the lens model, and establishes the robustness of our free-form lensing method for measuring model-independent geometric source distances and for deriving objective central cluster mass distributions. After correcting for its magnification the luminosity of MACS0647-JD remains relatively high at M<SUB>UV</SUB> = -19.4, which is within a factor of a few in flux of some surprisingly luminous z \u2243 10-11 candidates discovered recently in Hubble blank field surveys.", "doi": "10.3847/1538-4357/835/1/44", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1608.06942", "issn": "0004-637X", "number": "1"}, "Paper text": <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">GEOMETRIC CORROBORATION OF THE EARLIEST LENSED GALAXY AT <I>z</I>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a010.8 FROM ROBUST FREE-FORM MODELLING</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Brian M. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F835%2F1%2F44\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/835/1/44</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>A multiply lensed galaxy, MACS0647-JD, with a probable photometric redshift of <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$z\\simeq {10.7}_{-0.4}^{+0.6}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/apjaa4efcieqn1.gif\"></IMG> is claimed to constitute one of the very earliest known galaxies, formed well before reionization was completed. However, spectral evidence that MACS0647-JD lies at high redshift has proven infeasible and so here we seek an independent-lensing-based \"geometric redshift\" derived from the angles between the three lensed images of MACS0647-JD, using our free-form mass model (WSLAP+) for the lensing cluster MACSJ0647.7+7015 (at <I>z</I> = 0.591). Our lens model uses the nine sets of multiple images, including those of MACS0647-JD, identified by the CLASH survey toward this cluster. We convincingly exclude the low-redshift regime of <I>z</I>\u00a0&lt;\u00a03, for which convoluted critical curves are generated by our method, as the solution bends to accommodate the wide angles of MACS0647-JD for this low redshift. Instead, a best fit to all sets of lensed galaxy positions and redshifts provides a geometric redshift of <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$z\\simeq {10.8}_{-0.4}^{+0.3}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/apjaa4efcieqn2.gif\"></IMG> for MACS0647-JD, strongly supporting the higher photometric redshift solution. Importantly, we find a tight linear relation between the relative brightnesses of all nine sets of multiply lensed images and their relative magnifications as predicted by our model. This agreement provides a benchmark for the quality of the lens model, and establishes the robustness of our free-form lensing method for measuring model-independent geometric source distances and for deriving objective central cluster mass distributions. After correcting for its magnification the luminosity of MACS0647-JD remains relatively high at <I>M</I><SUB>UV</SUB> = \u221219.4, which is within a factor of a few in flux of some surprisingly luminous <I>z</I>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a010\u201311 candidates discovered recently in Hubble blank field surveys.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa4efcs1\" name=\"apjaa4efcs1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Cluster lensing has the advantage over field surveys of a magnification boost and also the ability to measure distances \"geometrically\" from the angular separations within each set of multiply lensed images (Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib5\">2005</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib70\">2014</a>). This ability provides a welcomed check of photometrically derived redshifts, particularly at high redshift where detections are weakest and restricted to fewer passbands, and for which a dusty-red galaxy solution at much lower redshift is usually feasible. For this purpose an accurate lens model is required, ideally based on many sets of multiply lensed images spread over the full range of source distances. Since the angles through which light is deflected scale with increasing source distance behind a given lens, we can convert these distances to source redshifts for a given cosmology. This \"geometric\" redshift can then be compared with independently derived photometric redshifts to discriminate between degenerate photometric redshift possibilities. This method has been applied to the large lensing cluster A1689 where geometric distances provided a consistency check of the lens model (Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib5\">2005</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib11\">2015a</a>; Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib38\">2007</a>), and for the recently claimed high redshift of a triple-lensed image at a photometric redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a09.6 (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib70\">2014</a>) behind A2744 in deep HFF data.</p><p>Currently, efficient detection of high-redshift galaxies is best achieved using the IR channel of the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em><em>)</em> Wide-Field Camera-3 (WFC3), supported by the <em>Spitzer Space Telescope</em>'s Infrared Array Camera (IRAC) in the mid-IR. This combination is now generating statistically useful samples of dropout galaxies to <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a08 in several independent deep-field surveys (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib45\">2010</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib46\">2013</a>; Ellis et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib21\">2013</a>; Finkelstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib23\">2013</a>; Schmidt et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib59\">2014</a>; Holwerda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib31\">2015</a>). Examples include high-redshift galaxies from the CANDELS (Grogin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib29\">2011</a>; Koekemoer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib36\">2011</a>) survey where relatively strong [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] and H<em>\u03b2</em> emission creates a mid-IR excess to provide the highest redshift spectroscopically confirmed galaxies (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib48\">2015</a>) reaching <em>z</em> = 8.68 (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib69\">2015b</a>), with confirming strong Ly<em>\u03b1</em> emission from Keck IR spectroscopy. Lensing by galaxy clusters has provided a number of even higher redshift candidates, with the highest redshift lensed galaxy being the triply lensed small round object MACS0647-JD that lies an estimated photometric redshift of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn3.gif\" alt=\"$z\\simeq {10.7}_{-0.4}^{+0.6}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">z\\simeq {10.7}_{-0.4}^{+0.6}</script></span></span> (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib10\">2013</a>), discovered behind the massive lensing cluster MACSJ0647.7+7015 (Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib20\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib20\">2007</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib65\">2011</a>), followed by a similar object at <em>z</em> = 9.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.2 (Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib64\">2012</a>) behind the CLASH cluster MACS1149+22. Deeper Hubble Frontier Field imaging (HFF), with 70 orbits of optical/IR imaging per cluster, has revealed a <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a010.1 candidate (Infante et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib32\">2015</a>) in the cluster MACS J0416-2403, likely to be one the faintest galaxies to date. In addition, another triply imaged galaxy has been uncovered by the HFF at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a09.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.3, magnified by a factor of \u00a020, by the lensing cluster A2744, that is supported geometrically in Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib70\">2014</a>) with our free-form model.</p><p>Deep Hubble grism observations by Pirzkal et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib50\">2015</a>) of MACS0647-JD do not detect emission lines at <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a02, adding weight to the higher redshift interpretation. Hubble grism observations have also detected the Lyman-break of a relatively bright galaxy GD11.1 at <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$z\\simeq {11.09}_{-0.12}^{+0.08}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">z\\simeq {11.09}_{-0.12}^{+0.08}</script></span></span> by Oesch et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib47\">2016</a>). This may strengthen the case for several other relatively bright and high-redshift galaxies claimed in the range <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a010\u201311, in the combined deep-field analysis of Holwerda et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib31\">2015</a>), from which GD11.1 (originally called GN-z10-1 in Holwerda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib31\">2015</a>, it had an estimated photometric redshift of <em>z</em> = 10.1) was identified. Placing this object at <em>z</em> = 11.1 implies an exceptional brightness of <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub> = \u221222.1\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.2 and this object is marginally resolved, so dominant AGN emission can be excluded. The luminosities of all six of the Holwerda et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib31\">2015</a>) objects are exceptionally high, and it is interesting that there seem to be an absence of lower luminosity galaxies at this redshift that could have been detected (Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib47\">2016</a>). This tendency toward unexpectedly high luminosity may conceivably indicate that <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a011 represents the earliest limiting time that galaxies formed, with nothing detected beyond. This conclusion is provisional at the moment, given that <em>HST</em>'s limit is effectively not far above <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/sime.gif\" alt=\"sime\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a012 for detecting Lyman-Break Galaxies (LBGs) in the longest wavelength 1.6 <em>\u03bc</em>m passband.</p><p>The improving quality of lensing data for clusters imaged with Hubble and the future with <em>James Webb Space Telescope</em> and EUCLID has encouraged us to focus on understanding and improving our free-form lensing method (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib17\">2005b</a>; Sendra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a>). We have applied this method to all of the HFF fields and some CLASH clusters, taking advantage of the increasing numbers of multiply lensed images and their photometric and spectroscopic redshifts as the input data (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib13\">2015b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib15\">2015c</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib12\">2016a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib14\">2016b</a>). We have made these models available to the community so that they may be compared to those derived by other teams. Parametric models may be regarded as best suited to virialized clusters (Halkola et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib30\">2006</a>; Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib38\">2007</a>) for which substructure is minimal but may be extended to accommodate obvious bimodal substructure, and confirmed in blind tests on an idealized bimodal model cluster (Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib42\">2016</a>). In general, however, the complexities of massive merging clusters such as those chosen for the HFF program require the definition of several new parameters for each additional model halo, where the definition of substructure and its location is less than objective, leading to ambiguity. It is important to have free-form capability even when modeling clusters with relaxed appearance, as these can contain levels of structure in detail locally affecting the position and brightness of individual lensed images, as we show here in this work and also in blind lensing comparison tests made by Meneghetti et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib42\">2016</a>) where a relaxed, simulated cluster with s typical level of substructure was found to be very well reproduced by the free-form methods, including WSLAP+ that we use here. Free-form modeling may be expected to excel relative to parametric models for the largest lenses, which are typically clusters in a state of collision with convoluted critical curves, including most of the HFF clusters for which WSLAP+ has proven capable of locating new sets of multiple images by virtue of its flexibility (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib11\">2015a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib14\">2016b</a>), and also in making successful predictions for the return of the \"Refsdal\" multiply lensed SN in HFF data (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib12\">2016a</a>; Treu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib63\">2016</a>) and for the intrinsic luminosity of a magnified SN1a in the HFF cluster A2744 (Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib55\">2015</a>).</p><p>The extent to which parametric lens models capture substructure and tidal distortions of the dark matter distribution, including relatively relaxed and merging clusters, has prompted us to look harder at the possibility of grid solutions, where the lens plane is represented by a uniform or an adaptive grid of Gaussian pixels. Early non-parametric studies with a simple uniform gridding of the mass distribution were not accurate enough for identifying new sets of multiple images because they did not have high enough resolution to capture the local perturbing effects of cluster member galaxies. Typically at least one member of any set of multiply lensed images will fall close to a member galaxy, with additional images often created in this way, for which the limited spatial resolution of a smooth grid does not capture. We have achieved a huge improvement recently in this approach by incorporating a simple model halo for each of the observed member galaxies, together with the smooth grid to model more distributed cluster mass in a uniform grid (Sendra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a>). Our simulations and successful applications of this improved free-form model have demonstrated that meaningful solutions to be found as the small scale deflections and additional multiple images locally generated by the member galaxies can be accounted for and new sets of multiple images can be identified.</p><p>We have demonstrated now with our HFF work that this free-form approach generates lens models that are sufficiently accurate to predict the locations of counter-images (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>) and can correct and complete ambiguous identifications reported in competing work, so that physically plausible mass distributions can be derived that are relatively free of model assumptions. The reliability of our method has been demonstrated with both simulated data (Sendra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a>) and observations of the lensing standard Abell 1689, (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib11\">2015a</a>) and our subsequent HFF work (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib13\">2015b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib15\">2015c</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib12\">2016a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib14\">2016b</a>). In particular we have stressed the ability of our models to account for the independent information contained in the relative fluxes of multiply lensed sources. We have shown that our models find a linear relationship between the predicted model magnifications and the observed fluxes for the multiply lensed sources (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>) lying symmetrically about the one of equality, with a dispersion consistent with the observed errors. Here we show that this constancy also holds here for our new free-form model of MACS0647+7015, adding great confidence in the objectivity of our free-form modeling of this cluster.</p><p>Previous lens models and input data for our lens model of MACS0647 are summarized in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs2\">2</a>. The image and photometric redshift source is presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs3\">3</a>, as well as detailed description of each of the nine lensed image systems in turn. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs4\">4</a> we describe our methodology of constructing a free-form lens model. We define \"geometric redshifts\" in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs5\">5</a>, based on the distance\u2013redshift scaling for multiply lensed galaxies. Our main results are described in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs6\">6</a>, with a demonstration on the predictive power on magnifications in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs7\">7</a>. The consistency check on geometric and photometric redshift comparisons are presented in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs8\">8</a>. We include the model prediction on extra lensed images in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs9\">9</a>, with discussions and conclusions in Sections <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs10\">10</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcs11\">11</a>, respectively. The mass model uncertainties are presented in the <xref summary=\"re presented in the Appendix. Standard cosmologi\">Appendix</xref>. Standard cosmological parameters are adopted: \u03a9<sub><em>M</em></sub> = 0.3, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub> = 0.7, and <em>h</em> = <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>/100 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> = 0.7.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa4efcs2\" name=\"apjaa4efcs2\">2.\u00a0AVAILABLE LENSING DATA AND PREVIOUS LENS MODELS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Here we rely on the CLASH survey for our input data when modeling MACSJ0647.7+7017. These data include the reduced images, photometry, BPZ-based photometric redshifts derived by the method of Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib10\">2013</a>), and positions of lensed images in Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib10\">2013</a>) and Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib65\">2011</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib68\">2015a</a>). There are a total of nine multiply lensed systems forming 24 identified images spread uniformly over the critical area of the cluster. The faintest image detected is around an AB magnitude of 28.5. Details of all images are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa4efct1\">1</a>. There are three previous lens models applied in Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib10\">2013</a>) for modeling this cluster, constituting both parametric and free-form methods. The \"light approximately traces mass\" method of Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib66\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib66\">2009a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib67\">2009b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib65\">2011</a>) has been proven successful in identifying over one thousand multiply lensed images in the CLASH survey and beyond. This method may be described as \"semi-parametric\" in that it is based initially on a superposition of dark matter for the visible cluster member galaxies and a smooth component is fitted to this with a general low-order polynomial fit to allow flexibility in the unknown dominant smooth cluster component. The member galaxies are then modeled by Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib65\">2011</a>) for MACS0647 simply as the residual of this cluster component after subtracting from the initial member galaxy composite, providing a minimalistic method that includes member galaxies and allows for some flexibility in the definition of a smooth cluster wide mass distribution following Broadhurst et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib5\">2005</a>). This method was developed to take advantage of the approximate relation expected between dark matter and the distribution of member galaxies that is generally close but not expected to be exact, due to tidal forces shaping the DM and the finite numbers of tracer galaxies that can be utilized.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa4efct1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nDetailed Information of Individual Lensed Images\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa4efct1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/suppdata/apjaa4efct1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/835/1/44\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/suppdata/apjaa4efct1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib10\">2013</a>) also make use of Lenstool (Kneib et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib34\">2011</a>)\u2014the most successful fully parametric method, which is best suited to symmetric lenses where the definition of the cluster mass distribution is not very ambiguous. In the case of merging asymmetric clusters there is the inherent problem with this tool of deciding how to deal with mass substructures within the cluster dark matter\u2014for each substructure that is introduced, at least six parameters must be added to the model including centroid, ellipticity, position angle, profile gradient, and amplitude, with an inherent restriction to a given class of such profiles. This model has been relied on for estimating the inherent magnification of the lensed sources, including the three counter-images of the object of interest here, MACSJ0647-JD.</p><p>Below we describe in greater detail the very general free-form lensing method that we have increasingly employed in our work on the HFF. This method takes advantage of the high density of multiply lensed images to provide sufficiently constraints on the smooth cluster mass distribution. The robustness and predictive power of the free-form method have been demonstrated by the capability we have to predict counter-images in the HFF data, and in some cases correcting the parametric model results of Lenstool in the case of the complex cluster A2744 (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>). Furthermore, because this method is largely model free, the geometric distances we derive are objective and not tied to any assumed cluster mass profile.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa4efcs3\" name=\"apjaa4efcs3\">3.\u00a0COLOR IMAGES AND PHOTOMETRIC REDSHIFTS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The retrieved drizzle science images were obtained from the online CLASH archive. The individually reduced images are produced by <em>Mosaicdrizzle</em> (Koekemoer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib35\">2013</a>) and publicly available.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcfn1\">10</a></sup>\n Each cluster was observed with a a wide filter coverage from 225 to 1600 nm. The images can probe faint galaxies to exceptional depth, and with their filter coverage photometrically identify galaxy candidates when the universe was younger than 800 million years old\u2014or less than 6% of its current age.</p><p>We generated a simple RGB image (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf1\">1</a>) by adding all filters that have been assigned to R, G, and B colors separately. The added images are combined in the publicly available software <em>Trilogy</em>.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efcfn2\">11</a></sup>\n\n</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa4efcf1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa4efcf1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0IDs of galaxies lensed to form multiple images by MACS0647+7015. In the process of generating this image, F160W and F140W construct the red color, F125W, F110W F105W, F850lp, F814W, and F775W contribute to green color, and F625W, F606W, F555W, F475W, and F435W are used in blue color. The field of view is 133<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />12\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0133<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />12. The separately modeled BCGs are circled in red.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa4efcf1\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa4efcf1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa4efcf1\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa4efcf1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The lensed image positions and redshifts were previously presented by Coe et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib9\">2012</a>). In Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib68\">2015a</a>), three more candidate systems were identified, referred to henceforth as systems 10 to 12. These three sources were not included in our mass model as we found the self-consistency of our mass model to be generally reduced after the addition. This reduction of self-consistency can be explained by either false identification of lensed images or insecure photometric redshifts. When we analyzed the self-consistency of the mass models, we found nine systems to be already sufficient in constraining the mass model to a level of precision that achieves excellent self-consistency.</p><p>We conducted our own photometry for each individual candidate lensed galaxy. Tailor-made apertures and sky annulus were applied to each multiply lensed images in a combined image of all WFC3 infrared bands (consisting of F105W, F110W, F125W, F140W, and F160W). We performed the photometry on a combined image of multiple bands as most lensed images are very faint. When the photometry is conducted on faint images, we used apertures approximately equal to the Rayleigh diffraction limit of the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>, which give us the highest S/N ratio.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa4efcs4\" name=\"apjaa4efcs4\">4.\u00a0FREE-FORM LENSING MODEL</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The free-form lensing method developed by Diego et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib16\">2005a</a>) is a grid-based iterative method that can be constrained by both strong and weak lensing information, including sets of individual pixels subtended by resolved arcs in the case of strong lensing. We have recently demonstrated that this method can be significantly improved by the addition of observed member galaxy deflections (Weak and Strong Lensing Analysis Package plus member galaxies: WSLAP+, Sendra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a>) because typically one or more counter-images of each multiply lensed system is either generated or significantly deflected by a local member galaxy. We have applied this method recently to the relaxed cluster A1689 (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib11\">2015a</a>), the HFF clusters A2744 (Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>), and MACS0416 (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib13\">2015b</a>). We have demonstrated that this combination of high- and low-frequency components can converge to meaningful solutions with sufficient accuracy to allow the detection of new counter-images for further constraining the lensing solution of A1689. This free-form method is especially useful for modeling the complex mass distributions of the merging clusters chosen for the Hubble Frontier Fields program. Parameterized models are inherently less useful in this context as the number of constraints is generally insufficint to model the additional parameters. We also apply this free-form method here as the structure of MACS0647+7015 is very elongated with some level of substructure anticipated and also because we wish to obtain relatively model-independent geometric redshift estimates for the lensed galaxies, particularly for MACS-JD, free from any restrictions inherent to idealized parametric profiles.</p><p>Here we outline briefly WSLAP+ for the mass reconstruction and refer the reader for details of its implementation in our previous papers (Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib16\">2005a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib17\">2005b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib18\">2007</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib11\">2015a</a>; Ponente &amp; Diego <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib52\">2011</a>; Sendra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a>).</p><p>Given the standard lens equation,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}\\,=\\,{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}-{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }},{\\rm{\\Sigma }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn8.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}</script></span></span> is the observed angular position of the source, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn9.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}</script></span></span> is the deflection angle, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn10.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Sigma }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Sigma }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})</script></span></span> is the surface mass density of the cluster at the position <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn11.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}</script></span></span>, and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn12.gif\" alt=\"${\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}</script></span></span> is the position of the background source, both the strong lensing and weak lensing observables can be expressed in terms of derivatives of the lensing potential</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\psi (\\theta )\\,=\\,\\displaystyle \\frac{4{{GD}}_{l}{D}_{{ls}}}{{c}^{2}{D}_{s}}\\int {d}^{2}\\theta ^{\\prime} {\\rm{\\Sigma }}(\\theta ^{\\prime} ){ln}(| \\theta -\\theta ^{\\prime} | ),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>D</em><sub><em>l</em></sub>, <em>D</em><sub><em>ls</em></sub>, and <em>D</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> are, respectively, the angular diameter distances to the lens, from the lens to the source, and from the observer to the source. The unknowns of the lensing problem are in general the surface mass density and the positions of the background sources. As shown in Diego et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib16\">2005a</a>), the lensing problem can be expressed as a system of linear equations that can be represented in a compact form,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\rm{\\Theta }}\\,=\\,{\\rm{\\Gamma }}X,\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where the measured lensing observables are contained in the array \u0398 of dimension <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn13.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{{\\rm{\\Theta }}}\\,=\\,2{N}_{\\mathrm{SL}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{{\\rm{\\Theta }}}\\,=\\,2{N}_{\\mathrm{SL}}</script></span></span>, the unknown surface mass density and source positions are in the array <em>X</em> of dimension <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn14.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{X}\\,=\\,{N}_{c}+{N}_{g}+2{N}_{s}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{X}\\,=\\,{N}_{c}+{N}_{g}+2{N}_{s}</script></span></span>, and the matrix \u0393 is known (for a given grid configuration and initial galaxy deflection field, see below) and has dimension <em>N</em><sub>\u0398</sub>\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0<em>N</em><sub><em>X</em></sub>. <em>N</em><sub>SL</sub> is the number of strong lensing observables (each one contributing with two constraints, <em>x</em>, and <em>y</em>), and <em>N</em><sub><em>c</em></sub> is the number of grid points (or cells) into which we divide the field of view (133<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />12\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0133<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />12), which equals 32<sup>2</sup> = 1024 in this case. <em>N</em><sub><em>g</em></sub> is the number of deflection fields (from cluster members) that we consider. <em>N</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> is the number of background sources (each contributes with two unknowns, <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>x</em></sub> and <em>\u03b2</em><sub><em>y</em></sub>, see Sendra et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a> for details). The unknowns are found after minimizing a quadratic function that estimates the solution of the system of Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efceqn3\">3</a>), with the constraint that the solution, <em>X</em>, must be positive, and is constrained not to generate sources that are unreasonably small given the angular sizes of faint galaxies resolved by Hubble. These constraints are particularly important to avoid the unphysical situation where the masses associated to the galaxies are negative (which could otherwise provide a reasonable solution, from the formal mathematical point of view, to the system of linear Equations (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efceqn3\">3</a>)). Imposing the constraint <em>X</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00 also helps in regularizing the solution as it avoids large negative and positive contiguous fluctuations.</p><p>For the model galaxy component we use the set of cluster member galaxies as used in Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib68\">2015a</a>). These galaxies are selected first from the red sequence, and then vetted by eye with a few obvious non-members excluded. From the <em>H</em>-band (F160W) magnitudes, a mass-to-light ratio of 20 <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>/<em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub> is initially assumed to construct the fiducial deflection field summed over the member galaxies, each having a truncated NFW profile (truncation radius equals scale radius times concentration parameter) with a scale radius linearly related to its FWHM in the NIR image. For our purpose, the exact choice of profile for member galaxies is not particularly important; what matters more is the normalization. This normalization is the only free parameter of the fiducial deflection field, and is determined by our optimization procedure. In Sendra et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib61\">2014</a>) we tested this addition to the method with simulated lensed images, but with the real galaxy members from A1689 to be as realistic as possible. We found that a separate treatment of the BCG lensing amplitude was warranted, adding a second deflection field i.e., <em>N</em><sub><em>g</em></sub> = 2 (see definition of <em>N</em><sub><em>g</em></sub> above) to be solved for. Here we follow the same procedure as A2744 incorporating member galaxies and the BCGs separately. We also find significant improvements in the residual by leaving free the amplitude of bright galaxies that significantly perturb nearby lensed images. In total, we decomposed the fiducial deflection field into seven components, comprising the two central galaxies, five components of member galaxies near to different lensed images, and one component for all other member galaxies.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa4efcs5\" name=\"apjaa4efcs5\">5.\u00a0GEOMETRIC REDSHIFTS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The derived distances provide a very welcome check on redshifts derived photometrically, particularly at high redshifts where images are generally noisy and may be detected in only the longest wavelength passband. For this purpose an accurate lens model is required, based on many sets of multiply lensed images and ideally sampling a wide range of source distances so that the gradient of the mass profile can be constrained. The reduced deflection field scales with increasing source distance behind a given lens so that the separations in angle between images of the same system are larger for higher redshift sources. Therefore, we have developed a code that can \"de-lens\" any particular image to the source plane, and \"re-lens\" it back to the image plane. We repeat the procedures for a redshift range around the claimed redshift of the source. This procedure defines \"loci\" along which we search for counter-images. By comparing the loci with the observed positions of counter-images, we obtain geometric distance estimates for each set of multiple images. Distances derived this way can then be converted via cosmological parameters to source redshifts and compared with independently derived photometric redshifts. This method has been established using the large lensing cluster A1689, where geometric distances provided a consistency check of the lens model (Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib5\">2005</a>; Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib38\">2007</a>; Lam et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib37\">2014</a>; Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa4efcbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa4efcbib11\">2015a</a>).</p><p>A lens model is a deflection field, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn15.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})</script></span></span>, that expresses the angle through which light is bent at the lens plane. An observer sees a reduced angle scaled by a ratio involving lens and source distances:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn4\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn4.gif\" alt=\"Equation (4)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})\\,=\\,\\displaystyle \\frac{{d}_{{ls}}(z)}{{d}_{s}(z)}{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}).\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>As can be observed from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efceqn4\">4</a>), the angles between the unlensed source and the lensed images slowly increase with source distance behind a given lens. This dependence means that the locations of a given set of multiple images will meet most closely in the source plane at a preferred source distance. In principle, we can only determine relative distances this way because the absolute value of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn16.gif\" alt=\"${{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({\\boldsymbol{\\theta }})</script></span></span> cannot be determined independently of lensing. By normalizing the model deflection field using a spectroscopic redshift measured for any one of the multiply lensed systems, the relative distances can be converted to absolute distances for a given cosmological model. In other words, what we actually determine, for the <em>k</em>th set of multiple images for a given lens, is the ratio of lensing distances:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn5\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn5.gif\" alt=\"Equation (5)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{f}_{k}(z)\\,=\\,\\displaystyle \\frac{{d}_{{{ls}}_{k}}(z)}{{d}_{{s}_{k}}(z)}/\\displaystyle \\frac{{d}_{{{ls}}_{o}}({z}_{o})}{{d}_{{s}_{o}}({z}_{o})}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where the <em>o</em> index refers to the multiply lensed system with spectroscopic redshift.</p><p>Considering an image <em>i</em> from source <em>k</em>, we can write the lens equation as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn6\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn6.gif\" alt=\"Equation (6)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{{\\boldsymbol{\\beta }}}_{k}\\,=\\,{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i}-\\displaystyle \\frac{{d}_{{{ls}}_{k}}(z)}{{d}_{{s}_{k}}(z)}{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i}),\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>Taking the difference of Equation\u00a0(<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efceqn6\">6</a>) from image <em>i</em> and <em>j</em>, we could eventually get this expression:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn7\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn7.gif\" alt=\"Equation (7)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&\\displaystyle \\frac{{d}_{{{ls}}_{k}}}{{d}_{{s}_{k}}}\\,=\\,\\displaystyle \\frac{({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,j})\\cdot ({{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i})-{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,j}))}{{[{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i})-{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,j})]}^{2}}\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>\n</p><p>We are free to normalize the <em>f</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> ratios as convenient, and this is sensibly done relative to a multiply lensed source with a reliable spectroscopic redshift, which is not available for this cluster, so we adopt the maximum value of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn17.gif\" alt=\"${d}_{{ls}}/{d}_{s}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{d}_{{ls}}/{d}_{s}</script></span></span> for a source at infinity, and with the lens redshift equal to that of the cluster <em>z</em> = 0.591. For a system involving multiple images, we can calculate the statistical mean of all the combinations of image <em>i</em>,<em>j</em>:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa4efceqn8\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efceqn8.gif\" alt=\"Equation (8)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{f}_{k}\\,=\\,\\displaystyle \\frac{2}{{n}_{k}({n}_{k}-1)}\\displaystyle \\sum _{i,j,i\\gt j}\\displaystyle \\frac{({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i}-{{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,j})\\cdot ({{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i})-{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,j}))}{{[{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,i})-{{\\boldsymbol{\\alpha }}}_{L}({{\\boldsymbol{\\theta }}}_{k,j})]}^{2}}.\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>In this expression, we compare the right expression, which depends on the \"goodness\" of our model, with the theoretical <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn18.gif\" alt=\"${d}_{{ls}}(z)/{d}_{s}(z)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{d}_{{ls}}(z)/{d}_{s}(z)</script></span></span> curve to evaluate the reliability of our lens model. Below we present a comparison of <em>f</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> values in mass models generated by assuming different redshifts of MACS0647-JD.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa4efcs6\" name=\"apjaa4efcs6\">6.\u00a0RESULTS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa4efcs6-1\" name=\"apjaa4efcs6-1\">6.1.\u00a0Low-redshift Solution</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>To examine the geometric constraints on the redshift of JD, we reconstructed the mass model of the cluster using all the input data in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa4efct1\">1</a> but allowing for a wide range of input redshift for system 6. The results are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf2\">2</a> with assumed redshifts for JD of <em>z</em> =  2, 4, 6, 8, 10, and 10.8. It is clear from this that the free-form model is capable of great versatility in fitting all the multiple image data, as can be seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf3\">3</a> where we plot the critical curve for a lensed system at <em>z</em> = 3 by setting <em>z</em> = 2 for system 6. With this low redshift, the lensing mass contours of the model greatly deviate from the observed distribution of central cluster members, with correspondingly very convoluted critical curves. The critical curves shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf3\">3</a> can be completely excluded as they cross each other in multiple places. It is very clear where the critical curves lie from the pattern of observed close pairs of multiple images and large arcs, matching closely the elongated distribution of member galaxies (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf1\">1</a>), apparent in the higher redshift solutions in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf2\">2</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa4efcf2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa4efcf2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Projected mass contours of our lens model generated for a range of different redshifts of system 6, from <em>z</em> = 2 to <em>z</em> = 10.8. The contours are spaced linearly in 14 steps. This shows how the model requires a significantly more structured mass distribution for a lower choice of redshift for system 6, particularly near the outlying image 6c, becoming generally much smoother for a choice of <em>z</em> = 11 for system 6. Note that in making these models all other eight multiply lensed systems at lower redshift are placed at their best photometric redshifts\u2014ranging from 1.9\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a06.5, with only system 6 varied in redshift between these plots.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa4efcf2\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa4efcf2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa4efcf2\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa4efcf2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa4efcf3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa4efcf3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Critical curves generated when assuming a low redshift, <em>z</em> = 2, for system 6 (MACS0647-JD) are shown in the upper panel, and the same plot assuming <em>z</em> = 11 in the lower panel. These critical curves cross each other in multiple places unlike the observed critical curves that follow a much simpler elliptical pattern like the distribution of the member galaxies and are delineated clearly by the observed close pairs of multiple images and large arcs (see Figure <a href=\"#apjaa4efcf1\">1</a>). these critical curves are overlaid on a plane of small evenly spaced sources to illustrate further the large differences between the low-redshift <em>z</em> = 2 solution (top) and the best-fit model with system 6 at <em>z</em> = 11 with color-coded diagonal stripes linking related multiple images. It is impressive that the free-form approach is capable of testing this low redshift, providing a clear negative answer.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa4efcf3\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa4efcf3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa4efcf3\" href=\"/0004-637X/835/1/44/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa4efcf3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Using the same set of mass models as shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf2\">2</a>, we can also compare our model solutions in terms of the values of the source distances that the model produces for each set of multiple images. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf4\">4</a>, the green points represent our model-predicted <em>f</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> curve assuming different redshift from 2 to 10.8 of JD. There each point represents the best <em>f</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> using Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa4efceqn8\">8</a>) for all the images of each system, while the blue curve represents the theoretical <em>d</em><sub><em>ls</em></sub>/<em>d</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> curve derived via the standard cosmological parameters. The derived values of <em>f</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> are in very good agreement with their expected values given by the blue curve, except for system 3, which falls far from the line when system 6 is assigned a low redshift. The reason for this discrepancy is that the images of system 3 lie very close to system 6 such that the model cannot simultaneously accommodate both systems if they lie at low redshifts. The lower redshift photometric solution for system 6 generates a contradiction for the model by approximately 2<em>\u03c3</em>, which is alleviated by setting system 6 to higher redshifts where it comes into very good agreement with the theoretical relation. Our best mass model assuming JD at <em>z</em> = 10.8 is shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf5\">5</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf7\">7</a>.<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf6\"> </a>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa4efcf7\"> </a>\n</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa4efcf4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa4efcf4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcf4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Lensing distant ratio, <em>f</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> (green dots) derived by the model for each multiply lensed system plotted against their photometric redshifts, where we explore a range of redshift for system 6, as indicated on each panel. The blue line shows the theoretical <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/835/1/44/Full/apjaa4efcieqn19.gif\" alt=\"${d}_{{ls}}(z)/{d}_{s}(z)$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{d}_{{ls}}(z)/{d}_{s}(z)</script></span></span> curve, for the standard cosmology which is very well fitted by the data in general, especially for a high redshift of system 6. The sensitivity of this fit is strongest for system 3, which lies relatively close to MACS0647-JD and improves as the redshift of MACS0647-JD is increased. Error bars of the <em>y</em>-axis represent the standard deviation of 46 model reconstructions each with different grid resolutions, initial conditions, and photometric redshifts assumptions (see <xref summary=\"ts assumptions (see Appendix). Error bars of the\">Appendix</xref>). Error bars of the <em>x</em>-axis represent 2<em>\u03c3</em> dispersion of photometric redshifts as presented in Coe et al.
